### Post by happysmileman on 2008-09-01
> **mips said:**
> Hopefully it is done in Qt4.

I agree, not just because of KDE themes and stuff, Opera uses Qt4 and looks fine in Gnome too, Google could obviously achieve that if they wish as well.

Also I feel that Qt is just a superior toolkit to GTK in many ways, would you really dislike if it was like Skype or Opera and had it's theme work perfectly well on Gnome, but was still Qt?

I've seen many Qt apps that looked fine on Gnome, every GTK app I've ever used has looked horrible in KDE.

EDIT: I'm just assuming that the main problem is themes, since my main problem with GTK is that they look terrible on KDE. Which doesn't affect Qt with the proper effort done by the developers of the program, it may not fit into your Gnome theme but it would look a lot better than a GTK program does on KDE

### Post by jsast21 on 2008-09-02
> **rune0077 said:**
> *Some* IE users don't know that IE is a web browser. Let's not generalize to much. I don't think I've ever met anybody who didn't know that (though I've met many who wasn't aware that there's alternatives to IE).

You've never met my father :-) 

As hard as I try he just is not interested in understanding the the how or the why. As long as his laptop turns on, "the internet" comes up when he clicks the big blue e, and his homepage is yahoo mail, he's good. 

There are many people like this. I have run across quite a few who have (for example) asked me to help them "fix their internet" when they have deleted the shortcut from their windows desktop, etc.

IMHO Google will not reach this market becuase 'Install a new Browser' or 'Install a new Internet' will not even catch their eye. They will either be too afraid of breaking something, or they will ask, "what's wrong with my old internet? it works fine".

But I love google and am so excited for the linux release.  But until then, I will also be trying it in Wine.

### Post by rune0077 on 2008-09-02
> **jsast21 said:**
> You've never met my father :-) 

As hard as I try he just is not interested in understanding the the how or the why. As long as his laptop turns on, "the internet" comes up when he clicks the big blue e, and his homepage is yahoo mail, he's good. 

There are many people like this. I have run across quite a few who have (for example) asked me to help them "fix their internet" when they have deleted the shortcut from their windows desktop, etc.


I understand that these people are there, I just don't think they cover a majority of people. You really do get browsers from many sources these days: cell-phones, palms, playstations, etc. As long as these all use different browsers, people who use them also have to accept that the "Internet" can be reached through many more means than just IE. I always end up making this argument, but I think it comes down to a generation thing. Yes fathers, depending on their age and profession, may be in the dark about these things. But little brothers and sisters and the more tech-savvy youth is not going to be so ignorant.

### Post by iheartubuntu on 2008-09-02
For all of us that are bored and have read everything we possible can about Google Chrome...

[http://blogs.ft.com/techblog/2008/09/top-ten-thoughts-on-google-chrome/](http://blogs.ft.com/techblog/2008/09/top-ten-thoughts-on-google-chrome/)

Herewith, 10 initial thoughts on Google’s new Chrome browser, based on its cartoon introduction:

1. Why a cartoon? What happened to the YouTube explanatory video? Are online cartoons the next big thing and is Google Chrome the red herring?

2. It’s a major rethink of the browser and a much more fully formed product than the usual Google beta.

3. Why couldn’t they work with Mozilla on this? Answer: they need more control over the user experience and their ability to serve ads - Microsoft and Mozilla have been taking some of that away with their latest releases.

4. Google makes a lot of solving the problem of browsers crashing when too many tabs are open and a javascript fails to execute- but who, other than superusers, really considers this a problem?

5. This is very OS-like - even down to imitating the Windows operating system Task Manager.

6. There is a fuller integration of Google Gears, making it easier for Google applications and the browser to replace the Windows desktop.

7. The address bar innovation seems aimed at combating Firefox’s similar “awesome” bar, which must have taken some search queries and advertising dollars away from Google.

8. Privacy innovations seem to match Microsoft’s “porn mode” in IE8.

9. Google says it is making the browser open source so others can take advantage of its innovations, but it needs to do so to make other browsers more compliant with its business.

10. Could this comic book be the cue for a geek superhero movie?

---

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-02
> **shirteesdotnet said:**
> 
1. Why a cartoon? What happened to the YouTube explanatory video? Are online cartoons the next big thing and is Google Chrome the red herring? Why not just a static page you can skim with appropriate bold type for emphasis? I'm impressed with the work Scout McCloud did, but it's not very efficient for getting information across to readers. And most people (i.e., not programmers or power users) will be confused by the third page anyway.

> 
3. Why couldn&#8217;t they work with Mozilla on this? Answer: they need more control over the user experience and their ability to serve ads - Microsoft and Mozilla have been taking some of that away with their latest releases. I thought the comic strip made it quite clear they wanted to start from the ground up. The changes they're making are so revolutionary, it'd take too long to change Firefox into that.

> 4. Google makes a lot of solving the problem of browsers crashing when too many tabs are open and a javascript fails to execute- but who, other than superusers, really considers this a problem? Everyone who uses more than one tab or who has had an error come about from Javascript considers it a problem. They may not know the source of the problem, but people are, of course, bothered by any performance hit.

> 9. Google says it is making the browser open source so others can take advantage of its innovations, but it needs to do so to make other browsers more compliant with its business. And that's wrong how?

### Post by iheartubuntu on 2008-09-02
Im using it now in my XP on Virtual Box. Looks nice. Simple. Basic... imported all my Firefox data in a matter of a second or two. Scrolling is quick.

There are two buttons to the right of the input bar... "control the current page" with an icon of a piece of paper w/ folded corner, and also the icon of a wrench titled "customize & control google chrome". Theres not a heck of a lot of options. CTRL+SHFT+N puts you incognito mode... pages you look through there "will not appear in your browser history or search history."

So far I like. Its simple, downloaded my FF links and link bar so Im happy. Too bad they didnt make it able to use Firefoxes apps :)

No problem:

- It's freakin' blue. I HATE BLUE. Nothing on my desktop is blue. I'm sick of blue. And I can't change the color. It sticks out like a sore thumb.

- No Skins like in Opera. 

- No saving open tabs when you close it, nor when it crashes.

- Bookmark browsing is slower than in Opera. You have to scroll if you have lots of bookmarks. Opera just opens a huge list. If you want more, just move your mouse over the arrow at the bottom and you have more. Nice and fast bookmark finding.

I think I'll be sticking to Opera for now. I'm guessing Chrome will be just as fast as Opera on my home PC. There really isn't any compelling reason to switch to Chrome (yet).

### Post by Kernel Sanders on 2008-09-02

### Post by Tindytim on 2008-09-02
I'm using it at the moment(XP). It does spell checking for me, and it does seems to be faster.

One thing that does seems rather odd to me is that in both IE and Firefox I had the option to actually select which sites I would accept cookies from when they tried to get them onto my system. Chrome doesn't have this option, so I feel slightly out of place.

Another thing that I found odd is the fact that the mouse wheel sensitivity appears to be higher. It makes me feels like I'm browsing faster when I can scroll to the bottom of the page faster.
Very interesting effect.

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-02
I don't question it.

There's absolutely no reason it *won't* beat Firefox.

You can't fault it on the closed source/open source front, so open source zealots won't have anything stopping them from switching.

And for the rest of the folks who don't care about closed source/open source, Chrome has the most important factor playing in its favor: name-brand recognition.

People already use GMail, Google Docs, Google Calendar, Google Maps... adding another Google program into their lives seems like a natural next step.

Not to mention that it's lightning-fast.

It seems to take the best from Firefox and Opera and then add some of its own stuff. I've already replaced Firefox with it on my Windows computer at work. As soon as the Linux port is out, Chrome will be my default browser on Ubuntu at home.

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2008-09-02
> **LaRoza said:**
> I do not live in the future. If this is going to take backseat to the Windows version, then it may be of less quality.
Mozilla / XUL, anyone? Poke at that with xwininfo and prepare to be thoroughly disappointed with that browser's Linux support. Indeed, releasing for everything at once - building one size for all - is not always the best way. Firefox runs everywhere but integrates nowhere (maybe a little bit in Windows). At least Google uses a real UI toolkit from time to time. (Granted, that does not seem to be the case here for the main interface, but at least it is for Options and the like).

Anyhow, LaRoza: Because, as you say, open source people all use Linux, they would all help port the browser to Linux and maintain it there if they are interested. That is good for everyone. For us because it creates fallbacks for the browser development, for Google because it eases costs, and for the development community because it gets people contributing.
I think this is a good first step and am glad by this because it means the different platforms will each see some special flavour.

The tabs at the top (above window contents) remind me very much of Fluxbox. I hope they catch on like Mozilla's did, because then we can properly annihilate tabs and implement them in the window manager as they should always have been.

The address bar is neat. It's like the awesome bar, but awesom*er*! I particularly like how it learns search boxes just based on form fields the user has filled out (then has them appear in the address bar), without the user actually needing to trigger anything. I think the search results and static pages need to be split, however, like Epiphany's Smart Bookmarks.

### Post by SomeGuyDude on 2008-09-02
As for the Windows/Linux argument, let me say this:

When you say "open source", the vast majority of Windows users won't know what that means. When you're a Windows user (meaning your average one), then the phrase "ah crap it's a trial version" is part of your vocabulary. Closed-source is so common you don't think about it, and likely don't understand why it matters aside from "neat, it's free".

The entire Linux community is about open-source. There are forks of Firefox because of disputes over licensing and whatnot, there aren't any in Windows that I'm aware of because Windows users don't give two figs about open or closed source as long as it's free. Linux users, by and large, are big on open-source, so you can bet that something like this will attract more.

Look at your browser usage stats. IE still has the majority. Is that because it's a better browser? No, it's because it's default and a huge number of Windows users aren't interested in looking for a replacement, so right off the bat it's a safe bet that the 60% of internet users who stick with IE aren't even going to glance at Chrome.

I think it's a little foolish to limit this to Windows only for any period of time, just because I honestly think it'll get more support from Linux users at first, ESPECIALLY in the realm of bug-testing.

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-02
The question is whether Chrome will beat *Firefox*, not whether it will be *Internet Explorer*.

It will beat Firefox, barring some unpredicted major goof from the Google folks.

Defaults are powerful, but they are not insurmountable. I know a lot of non-tech-savvy Windows users who use iTunes instead of Windows Media Player.

Firefox has no brand-name recognition for Windows users. Google, however, has huge brand-name recognition. If they paste "Download Chrome Now" on the Google main page, there'll be a ton of downloads. People always get excited about new Google products (GMail, Google Docs, Google Earth, Google Maps).

I'm not saying every Windows user will, but a lot more of them will try out a Google browser than will try Firefox or Opera.

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2008-09-02
Of course Chrome doesn't have any spyware or advertising. What part of being open is not understood here?!

I can think of many reasons for Google to do this:

[LIST]
[*]Beating back Internet Explorer's majority market share, thus lessening Microsoft's grasp on the interwebs.

[*]To get a decent web browser because Google employees do not like what is out there. (This it the only browser to date I have thoroughly liked, because its design makes it invisible).

[*]To make Google's name present in applications beyond google.com.

[*]To encourage standards compliance. Google likes standards compliance for many reasons, one of which being that proper HTML means better search indexing.

[*]To encourage WebKit, which is the only decent web rendering engine and of use to Android.
[/LIST]

I was quite impressed when Chrome first started up, because it told me that Google was set to be my search provider and immediately asked me if I wanted to change that. Not only did it have that option, but it had all of Google's competitors (even the stronger ones) listed as choices.

---

It is *not* just a Windows thing.

### Post by spupy on 2008-09-02

### Post by happysmileman on 2008-09-02
Well the main speed advantages would be that it uses Webkit and V8 (the new javascript VM), those won't go away or slow down no matter how much features are added to the browser.

There's also the fact that different tabs/scripts/plugins each run in their own process, this might not actually make it faster by much, if any, but it means that the browser UI doesn't lock up when a heavy script is running, only that tab does, all other tabs are ready to accept your input and respond.

---

### Post by ionoff on 2008-09-02
First of all, Google Chrome is Google's new web browser.
It has lots of "nifty", yes I said `nifty`, features.

It was just released today for beta.

Unfortunately, it is only available for Win32.

But... what is availiable for Linux is the Chronium ([http://dev.chromium.org](http://dev.chromium.org)), which is kinda a unbranded Google Chrome in progress. [http://dev.chromium.org/developers/how-tos/build-instructions-linux](http://dev.chromium.org/developers/how-tos/build-instructions-linux)
[http://code.google.com/p/chromium/](http://code.google.com/p/chromium/)
[http://blog.chromium.org/](http://blog.chromium.org/)
Issue List: [http://code.google.com/p/chromium/issues/list](http://code.google.com/p/chromium/issues/list)

It does not work with the Latest build of Wine.
It uses the Doc and Settings App Settings folder and some other fun stuff.

Right now it is just unit tests and a few things, but it is very easy to build on Gutsy.

If you want google to tell you when it is released, just visit [http://www.google.com/chrome/intl/en/linux.html](http://www.google.com/chrome/intl/en/linux.html) and put in your email address.

The official page is:
[http://www.google.com/chrome](http://www.google.com/chrome)

Info about it:
[http://www.google.com/googlebooks/chrome/](http://www.google.com/googlebooks/chrome/) (Warning Comic Book)
[http://www.google.com/chrome/intl/en/features.html](http://www.google.com/chrome/intl/en/features.html)
[http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JGmO7Oximw8&fmt=18](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JGmO7Oximw8&fmt=18)
[http://googleblog.blogspot.com/2008/09/fresh-take-on-browser.html](http://googleblog.blogspot.com/2008/09/fresh-take-on-browser.html)
[http://twitter.com/googlechrome](http://twitter.com/googlechrome) (Ugly Twitter Page with links to other links and content)

### Post by basotl on 2008-09-02
> **Snoophyx said:**
> [This page]("http://dev.chromium.org/developers/how-tos/build-instructions-linux") describes how to build the Chromium browser in Linux. Read this if you're interested in testing the build or porting code.

**On that page:**
Note: There is no working Chromium-based browser on Linux. Although many Chromium submodules build under Linux and a few unit tests pass, all that runs is a command-line "all tests pass" executable.

### Post by OldGamer on 2008-09-02
When I first heard about chrome, I really didn't care.  But I looked into it for a while and must admit I got just as excited as everyone else.  Since I'm still dual booting, I downloaded google chrome as soon as I could, and I have been extremely impressed so far.  I loved the create application option because it makes it easy to set up my 3 year old daughters game sites.

Anyway, I have seen many people talk about google tracking everything that you do in their search engine and their new browser.  My question is, does this really matter?  As far as google is concerned, I'm just a random ip number, so how could it affect me in the least if they know what sites I like?  I'd like to know what other people really think about this and how it affects them.  Am I just really naive thinking it is no big deal?  Any thoughts on the subject are appreciated.

OldGamer

---

### Post by joninkrakow on 2008-09-03
> **phrostbyte said:**
> 
Edit: And they already have an operating system they are writing, for mobile devices. So it isn't much a stretch for a Google desktop OS.

What I'm looking for is a scalable OS. Same OS, same "computer" but when it's displaying on a handheld, it uses a touch-screen interface, and scales to the smaller screen size (maybe no menubars, one app-at-a-time). When running on a laptop, with a smaller screen, it scales to that, also enables elements that work fine on the more capable processor (maybe even with a unified menubar like OSX), and when it is run on a full-blown desktop, multiple monitors, full-bore power, it takes full advantage of that environment, and scales to that as well. Apple could have done that, what with the iPhone, etc. but they haven't. Google can also do this, what with Android--but I bet they don't.... I guess it's too difficult to implement today? Bummer... Imagine having your "computer" on a micro-SD card. Plug it into your phone, and it's a phone. Plug it into your laptop, and it's a laptop. Plug it into your desktop, and it's your desktop. Flush it down the toilet accidently while putting it into your phone, and you're toast.... hm.... maybe that's why we'll never see it...

;-)

-Jon

### Post by Game_boy on 2008-09-03
I really like it - it's the first browser since Firefox to make me want to set it as default. I've yet to find a site that doesn't work, and the only 'flaw' is it doesn't take plugins so I can't use NoScript-like functions.

It's sort of like Epiphany in design (minimalist but functional) except it feels modern and has a lot of unique features that actually mean something to the average user. The commitment to open-source principles (modularity so everyone can benefit; BSD license; use of Google Code; etc.) is impressive too. It could even become Ubuntu's default browser.

If you can try it, then do.

### Post by Game_boy on 2008-09-03
> **Giant Speck said:**
> That's because the website detects what operating system you are using.  My guess is that if you wanted to try it in wine, you need to log into Windows, download the .exe and put it on some kind of portable media and then log back into Ubuntu and paste it there.

### Post by paulstaf on 2008-09-03
I was on their webcast yesterday and from the way they were talking it sounds like the Firefox guys and the Google guys are very close in proximity to each other out there and that the collaboration will probably stay the same as both products are open source.  (Firefox / Chrome).

 
I used it all day yesterday and I didn't have any more crashes or issues than I would on a normal Windoze box with IE or FF.  I was hitting all the pages I know to cause me grief and they all worked rather well and faster than before.

I love running my email app in the "application" window mode.  More real estate for my stuff.

The person that was concerned earlier in this post about his personal data...Chrome appears to use your cache and history files to create a little profile so that they can provide you with a personalized page of things you normally do on the web.  I am sure that IE and FF keep the same information in your cache and history, its just that they don't do anything real extraordinary with it like Chrome does with the personalized page.

Google said that they didn't want people to think they were the 'big brother' or that your usage would be tracked by them.  It would be easy enough to do a packet sniff to make sure, but I doubt Chrome would make it as a browser very long if they were logging your activity.  

Incognito mode is great for when I am checking my bank information at work and don't want any of it cached on my work puter.

IE is dead.  FF and Chrome will be the new competitors now.

### Post by graabein on 2008-09-03
Tried the beta on Windows XP at work today. I'm impressed. It seems very smooth and fast and the GUI is nice. I'm missing a few things though: 

[LIST]
[*]AdBlock
[*]Some kind of image block (also need a better plug-in for Firefox. Any suggestions?)
[*]Seemless integration with Delicious (local bookmarks are stupid!)
[*]Some problems with meebo.com (probably meebo's fault, won't stop loading...)
[*]Should be easier to switch spell checker.
[/LIST]

I recommend you try it but don't do a full switch just yet. Beta 0.2! ;)


**HOWTO Search your delicious tags** 

[LIST=1]
[*]Right-click address field and select *Edit search engines...*
[*]Add new;
[*]Name: delicious
[*]Keyword: del
[*]Url: [http://delicious.com/](http://delicious.com/)**username**/%s
[/LIST]

To search just open a new tab (Ctrl+T) and type in address (Ctrl+L) "del **tagname**" and hit enter.

:guitar:

### Post by billgoldberg on 2008-09-03
> **linuxlizard said:**
> 

Nice part is that it's open source- maybe we can expect speed improvements in firefox in the near future because of this.


I wouldn't count on it.

Chrome uses the open source Webkit engine to display content. Firefox uses it's own open source engine called Gecko.

Some other browsers that use webkit: konqueror, midori, safari

The new epiphany browser (the official gnome browser) will also use Webkit, and that one has adblock plus and more add ons. 

I might be using that when it comes out instead of firefox or chrome.

### Post by Barrucadu on 2008-09-03
> 9.4 Other than the limited licence set forth in Section 11, Google acknowledges and agrees that it obtains no right, title or interest from you (or your licensors) under these Terms in or to any Content that you submit, post, transmit or display on, or through, the Services, including any intellectual property rights that subsist in that Content (whether those rights happen to be registered or not, and wherever in the world those rights may exist). Unless you have agreed otherwise in writing with Google, you agree that you are responsible for protecting and enforcing those rights and that Google has no obligation to do so on your behalf.

The *limited* license in section 11 is the key phrase here; for the license to make sense at all, section 11 must only apply to their web apps, otherwise it is contradictory, and doesn't make sense.

### Post by nvteighen on 2008-09-03
What bothers me a *a lot* is that Chrome is beta software. I don't like when a manufacturer announces something to the general public without having finished it... I mean, I'd rather released a snapshot for developers and beta-testers that know how to deal with issues, like Mozilla or Ubuntu do. But to launch a beta browser this way, with lots of people downloading it but without noticing what a "Beta release" means... well, I don't like it.

(btw, I didn't like when Hardy came out with FF 3rc5 either)

What if Chrome happens to have a major security vulnerability? If it was launched for beta-testing, well, the issue would not be that grave: at the end, that's what beta-testing is about.

Chrome is great from a technical point of view: the compiled Javascript, the process guard, etc., but I have already seen a frustrated user (at home). It's that different that people might have problems to switch to it...

For some reason, I feel Google will fail this time. I don't know why, being myself a Google-fan... The real features of Chrome are very technical, not perceivable to a regular user; yes, it will be a major contribution to developers because of being FOSS, but for the users?

I hope the IE-team has sent these guys a cake too, like they did with the Mozilla FF-team ;)

### Post by Kernel Sanders on 2008-09-03
PIPE DOWN SCAREMONGERS!!! :lolflag:

This is the typical licence for ALL Google services, not just Chrome. So if anyone of you doesn't like or accept this, you better get started deleting your gmail/blogger/googletalk/youtube/googlemaps/google desktop search/Google Apps etc.. accounts pretty quick, and you'll all stop using Google for web searches right? :rolleyes:

What Google is saying, in a nutshell, is that you have the copyright to everything you do using Google Chrome or any of their services, but in using their products you give them the right to use whatever you did to promote Google, such as in adverts etc..

BIG DEAL. It's this kind of clause that allows them to use a blog post you wrote on blogger as part of their advertising campaign for blogger if they ever want to.

*YAWN*. No problems with that here.

You are also forgetting that Google Chrome has no method of recording everything you do online and sending it back to Google. So this applies to Google Services mostly, and when you use a Google Service with Google Chrome for example.

Otherwise what your suggesting is that Google Chrome is recording what I type right now, and what site i'm at right now and sending it back to Google. If it's doing that with all users, Google will need another 50 Data Centres to collect all this useless information! :lolflag:

I really really don't see the problem with this. Google is providing a ton of free, very awesome services, and in exchange Google can use what we do to promote Google if they want to.

BIG DEAL. You're all acting like google just installed spyware on your PC's along with chrome, and they are going to use all your personal info in some diabolical way :rolleyes:

They didn't. This is the standard EULA for all online services.

### Post by Kernel Sanders on 2008-09-03
> **saulgoode said:**
> Why should I have to agree to anything with regard to using Google's services just to install a web browser?

The browser is part of their "services" (as defined in the EULA). Google Earth etc does exactly the same thing. 

All Googles services are tightly knit. Google told everyone the browser would call home, and they said why, and they ensrined that in the EULA.

There is nothing sinister here, nor underhand. 

Google are being totally upfront. You want to use Googles products and services, then you allow Google to collect some pretty harmless data if they choose to. It's not even a sure thing. In fact, you have more chance of winning the lottery than Google using that clause on YOU.

### Post by joninkrakow on 2008-09-03
> **Kernel Sanders said:**
> Scaremonger much? You OWN IT, just google can use part/all to promote google essentially. 


Actually, it's not to promote Google! It's simple, really. You own the copyright of your own data, right? Therefore, you own the right to limit/prohibit others' rights/capabilities to transmit and store your copyrighted material. 

Here's Google's problem. If you use their software and hardware, their software and hardware is being used to transmit and store *_your_* copyrighted material. Therefore, they *_must_* have the right to do so, in order to be legally transmitting *_your_* stuff. If you didn't, then you could sue them, because you stored your own photo (for instance) on their server.... seems ridiculous, but it would be possible. By agreeing in advance that they have that right to store and transmit your copyrighted info, they are protected from that eventuality--supposedly. I should add that I believe that in the terms of service for specific services (blogger, docs, etc.) Google specifically states that their license is valid only so long as your copyrighted material is on their hardware (meaning when you delete it, they no longer have a right to use/display it, etc.) 

In other words, the sole purpose for this license is so they can display what you've put on Google. It does not permit them to use your information for advertising, etc, just displaying. Where does the "promoting" come in? Simple. Suppose you post some amazing pictures, or a very popular post on blogger that draws a lot of traffic to blogger. Since blogger hosts your blog, you are, de-facto, promoting blogger. It's that simple. 

You know, this stuff has been discussed before, and lawyers have spoken up on this. I wish people would (ahem) Google(R) these things before flying off the handle. In any case, there's nothing to worry about, to be honest. But you're free to worry and fret if you prefer...

-Jon

### Post by klange on 2008-09-03
So I just tried Chrome out on my XP dual-boot, and I have to say: No. Absolutely not. Whatever they're doing - it's not helping.

First page I check out is naturally my home page, which I'll note runs on a server on the same network as the machine I'm browsing with. What's all this BS I hear about it being fast? It took forever to load a page on my own server!

Next stop was my localhost. Which didn't resolve. Typing 'localhost' into the "all-in-one" bar brought up a Google query for "localhost". Not what I'm looking for. (Same thing happened with such compliant names as 'oasis-games.local') Alright, let's try 127.0.0.1...

Finally. And you know what? I'm seeing scan lines on a fairly simple HTML 4.0 page. That's not good.

It also doesn't seem to have full zooming. That's odd. WebKit supports zooming images with text, right?

View Source tab title was stuck at "Loading..."



Honestly, I think all the things they're touting as being so special can all be implemented in Firefox (and without their help).

**e**: Hah! I'm even having problems reading their comic book because the javascript isn't running right.

### Post by mellowd on 2008-09-04
Not sure if this has already been posted: 

[http://news.cnet.com/8301-13860_3-10031703-56.html](http://news.cnet.com/8301-13860_3-10031703-56.html)

> Google said on Wednesday that it plans to alter contract terms that gave the search provider broad rights to use anything entered into its new Chrome browser.
"In order to keep things simple for our users, we try to use the same set of legal terms (our Universal Terms of Service) for many of our products," Google said in a statement provided to CNET News. "Sometimes, as in the case of Google Chrome, this means that the legal terms for a specific product may include terms that don't apply well to the use of that product. We are working quickly to remove language from Section 11 of the current Google Chrome terms of service."

### Post by ronnielsen1 on 2008-09-04
Note: There is no working Chromium-based browser on Linux. Although many Chromium submodules build under Linux and a few unit tests pass, all that runs is a command-line "all tests pass" executable.  [http://dev.chromium.org/developers/how-tos/build-instructions-linux](http://dev.chromium.org/developers/how-tos/build-instructions-linux)

### Post by saulgoode on 2008-09-04
From [http://news.cnet.com/8301-13860_3-10031703-56.html]("http://news.cnet.com/8301-13860_3-10031703-56.html"):

> Google said on Wednesday that it plans to alter contract terms that gave the search provider broad rights to use anything entered into its new Chrome browser.
"In order to keep things simple for our users, we try to use the same set of legal terms (our Universal Terms of Service) for many of our products," Google said in a statement provided to CNET News. "Sometimes, as in the case of Google Chrome, this means that the legal terms for a specific product may include terms that don't apply well to the use of that product. We are working quickly to remove language from Section 11 of the current Google Chrome terms of service."

Altering the contract terms still misses the point. Chrome is BSD-licensed Free Software -- ANY contract will result in a fork where the same software gets offered without a "contract" being required.

### Post by scragar on 2008-09-04
I ran it in a virtual box for about 30 seconds, it's appearance is horrible(oh, sure, someone will say it looks nice, but the thing is square when it should be smooth, corners and tabs being a big example), and I've found a few bugs already, firstly there's a limit on the number of tabs/window, (it's actually quite reachable), the problem, is that once you start closing these tabs the new tab button doesn't reappear till you get down to 3 tabs, smart behaviour, then there's a bug where some select URLs appearing in your bookmarks or history can cause serious problems with it's copycat to the firefox awesome bar, and the opera rip off om the quick dial.
My last complaint, is that the options are so sparse, I can't set cookies to be blocked on all but some sites, or change file handling, or even mess about with default fonts, all in all it's pretty rubbish for those of us wanting a customisable browser.

---

### Post by Onay on 2008-09-04
The Good: Sleek and simple. Designed with security in mind (each tab is it's own process that cannot access the rest of the browsing tabs). V8 Javascript engine is comparable with speed to firefox's Spidermonkey. Integrates well with a desktop though; Multifunction address bar is convenient. OPEN SOURCE!!

The Bad: Each tab seems to take up considerable amounts of memory. The V8 engine was benchmarked against the upcoming Firefox 3.1 TraceMonkey javascript engine and mozilla's engine is faster in all categories. Simple design makes customization difficult. No extensions or theming like firefox's addons further make it a simple browser without much extendability.

Bottom Line: The browser is excellent for simple use, but it does not have any marketable feature that sets it apart from the other big players (besides the Incognito (AKA Porn Viewing) mode which allows users to browse anonymously while not saving any history or cookies after the session). It's open source so there's plenty of potential, and I see a promising future with the possibilities of the best browser for OS and desktop integration.

---

### Post by scragar on 2008-09-05
```
wget http://www.kegel.com/wine/winetricks
chmod +x winetricks
sudo cp winetricks /usr/sbin
winetricks riched20 riched30 *flash*
winetricks allfonts

```flash is optional.

lauch installer and install.

### Post by forrestcupp on 2008-09-05
> **alt3rn1ty said:**
> 
Just curious but anyone know if incognito mode can be set as the default mode or wouldn't that work with the home page and profiled history of searches, with the ability to switch back to normal if desired?. Not just for privacy but seems to me the increased memory usage in each process/tab could be reduced by not using cache/history storage for multiple tabs.

Don't be overly comfortable about the privacy of incognito mode.  All it does is keep your history and all of that stuff from being saved on your computer.  Web sites can still see who you are.  There's no internet privacy or security with it.  Basically the only use for incognito mode is so your mommy can't see the nasty things you've been doing on the net.

Chrome is awesome.  Other than the lack of an ad blocker, the main thing I don't like is the lack of an option to automatically switch to a new tab.  When I click on a link, I don't want to have to go up and click on the tab just to see it.

### Post by rune0077 on 2008-09-05
Some news: [http://www.crn.com/software/210500053;jsessionid=I2HMCUSOX5DYYQSNDLPCKH0CJUNN2JVN](http://www.crn.com/software/210500053;jsessionid=I2HMCUSOX5DYYQSNDLPCKH0CJUNN2JVN)

> Even though Google's Chrome has been publically available for less than a week, its Web browser market share has already reached over 1 percent.

> On September 3, the day Chrome was announced and launched to the public, Firefox's market share dipped 0.82 percent, while Safari dropped 0.21 percent, giving Chrome a 1.11 percent share on the day. A day later, Chrome was up 0.04 percent while Firefox fell another 2.28 percent. Safari remained consistent with the day before, losing 0.22 percent.

Interestingly enough, the only browser that grew in the Web browser market was Internet Explorer, according to Stat Counter.

### Post by kirsis on 2008-09-07
> **LonelyTraveler said:**
> Has anyone here been able to use Chrome successfully in Ubuntu? I got it installed, but as soon as I go to a page it gives me a black screen.

Here's a howto: [http://appdb.winehq.org/objectManager.php?sClass=version&iId=13635](http://appdb.winehq.org/objectManager.php?sClass=version&iId=13635)

> 
First, get wine-1.1.4 (or build wine from git).

Then grab a fresh winetricks and .wine, and install the following packages (firefox only needed to download chrome):

cd $HOME
rm winetricks
wget [http://www.kegel.com/wine/winetricks](http://www.kegel.com/wine/winetricks)
mv .wine .wine.old
sh winetricks msxml3 corefonts firefox flash winxp
Using Firefox in Wine, download the online installer from chrome.google.com and let Firefox run it.

Or, if you already have Firefox installed, you can grab Chrome's installer like this:

wine .wine/drive_c/Program\ Files/Mozilla\ Firefox/firefox.exe chrome.google.com
Don't let Chrome's installer start Chrome. Instead, to run Chrome, do:

wine "$HOME/.wine/drive_c/windows/profiles/$USER/Local Settings/Application Data/Google/Chrome/Application/chrome.exe" --no-sandbox --new-http

### Post by chocbar31 on 2008-09-07

/home/USER/.wine/drive_c/windows/profiles/USER/Local Settings/Application Data/Google/Chrome/Application/0.2.149.29/Themes

Replace USER with your username!

### Post by vishzilla on 2008-09-08
[Google Search Webspam head Matt Cutts hinted this in a QnA]("http://www.mattcutts.com/blog/common-google-chrome-objections/")
> Q: But I can&#8217;t install extension X! Google Chrome is dead to me if I can&#8217;t use extension X!
A: Then you&#8217;ll have to use another browser for a while. Google Chrome currently doesn&#8217;t support browser extensions (it does support plug-ins, such as Flash). I&#8217;m sure that extensions/add-ons are something that the Chrome team would like to do down the road, but the Chrome team will be a bit busy for a while, what with the feedback from the launch plus working on Mac and Linux support. I&#8217;d suggest that you give Google Chrome a try for a few days to see if enjoy browsing even without extension X. A lot of really cool extension-like behaviors such as resize-able textareas and drag-and-drop file upload are already built into Google Chrome.

Edit: Google are serious about this browser. In one of those questions, he mentioned the Chrome team are as serious as Gmail

### Post by MatthewPlanchard on 2008-09-08
I installed Google Chrome on one of the university computers today and it is FAST!!

I have never seen pages load so fast, and opening up .pdf files happens instantaneously.  The ability to put a shortcut to an app on the desktop is really awesome, as well.

I am really, really impressed with Google Chrome on all fronts.

I cannot wait for a linux version so I can install this on my home computer.

Of course, Firefox is still incredible, but I think I'll be using Google for any browsing for which I don't need extensions.

Edit:  The V8 javascript engine performs with unparalleled speed.  Check out this website:

[http://celtickane.com/webdesign/jsspeed.php](http://celtickane.com/webdesign/jsspeed.php).

By comparison, Firefox scored nearly 2000 ms, Opera around 500.  Chrome scored 191 ms.

### Post by phaed on 2008-09-09
> **LonelyTraveler said:**
> Has anyone here been able to use Chrome successfully in Ubuntu? I got it installed, but as soon as I go to a page it gives me a black screen.

I used Ubuntu Geek's [directions]("http://www.ubuntugeek.com/install-google-chrome-with-wine-in-ubuntu.html"), but I found that it works better when running the command:

```
wine ~/.wine/drive_c/windows/profiles/$USER/Local\ Settings/Application\ Data/Google/Chrome/Application/chrome.exe --new-http --in-process-plugins
```

from terminal rather than replacing it in the menu item.  However, it IS still quite buggy.

### Post by indecisive on 2008-09-09

### Post by scragar on 2008-09-09
passing acid 3 doesn't make it a more standards compliant browser, it only says that it may handle a relativly small number of rules, most of which are used to handle bad code, rather than good code.

if you wanted to you could write a browser tha didn't pass acid3, but would enforce all of the standards, sure, it'd then fail on 2/3 of the internet, but that's becauase of bad coding all around...

### Post by RTrev on 2008-09-09
> **RTrev said:**
> A page that isn't finished yet, but will probably be quite nice for measuring browser percentages is:

[http://www.grc.com/cookies/stats.htm](http://www.grc.com/cookies/stats.htm)

This cookie project is temporarily on hold while a couple of higher-priority projects are being worked on, 

The numbers may not be completely representative of the general population, since mostly geeks go to Steve's site.  <g>

Bob

ADDENDUM

We've just realized that Chrome includes the word "Safari" in its USER-AGENT string, so the chart I pointed to above is currently collapsing both Chrome and Safari into one category!  Steve will fix that after he gets done recording the next SecurityNow episode tomorrow, and will break Chrome out into its own category as it should be.

One of the things that made us suspicious is that, in addition to the number of Safari users suddenly jumping by a large and inexplicable amount, we also saw that the Persistent Third Party cookie numbers for Safari, which is the only browser which currently comes with reasonable default settings, suddenly started looking almost as bad as all the other browsers which come set with poor defaults for handling third-party cookies.

Initial assessment is that Chrome "leaks" TP cookies.  Setting it to (I forget the strange wording they use) block TP cookies only prevents it from accepting *new* TP cookies, but any it already has get sent out even if TP cookies are supposedly disallowed.  Bad Chrome!  <g>

So, if we wait a couple or three days we'll see the actual usage and cookie handling of Chrome broken out correctly.. which should be interesting.

Bob

### Post by jasontu on 2008-09-11
I'm writing this on Chrome during my lunch break at work right now.  I like it a lot, but it's hard to judge since my network connection is kind of iffy.  

Sadly, it is true that this will pull more people from Firefox than IE.  The sheer weight of Google publishing their own browser though will necessitate web developers to make sites friendlier to IE alternatives.  

Google does support FireFox a great deal.  The purpose of Chrome though is to build something from scratch that will handle web content of the future without worrying about old code build-up.

Since it is open source, I see FireFox and Chrome converging at some point down the road.

The biggest step towards breaking IEs dominance (which is relatively weak compared to Windows dominance) is preventing it from being pre-loaded.

And yes, there are people do think of IE as "THE INTERNET."  There are people out there still who think that AOL is "THE INTERNET" and that all these other ISP are illegal knockoffs.

My parents aren't nearly that bad, but I did have to hide the IE icon deep in some folders and label the Firefox icon with the word INTERNET to get them to switch.

### Post by Dougie187 on 2008-09-11
> **Rashedul said:**
> lol .. um so what is the difference between a desktop url shortcut and a web application from Chrome or Prism on Firefox? I still don't get it and I've been searching the web since my last post ... (yes I'm bored)

I'm not sure if you mean a web application in general, or the web apps that google gear's supports. As far as that goes, a desktop url shortcut is simply what it sounds like, a shortcut on your desktop that opens up a url.

Where as the web apps are similar, in that they are shortcuts to urls, but you have to have google gears installed for it to work, and it basically gives you offline access to something you traditionally need to be online for. For example, they have an offline version of Google Docs, so it simply gives you an easy way to access your google docs offline and they sync them with your online copy when you are online.

### Post by SomeGuyDude on 2008-09-16
> **Kuzja said:**
> Just for the sake of argument, have a look here guys [http://www.osnews.com/story/20244/Google_Chrome_Considered_Harmful](http://www.osnews.com/story/20244/Google_Chrome_Considered_Harmful)

Looks like a hit job to me. The people who have used it say it uses less memory than Firefox (and two have said less than IE), so I'm not sure what this guy is basing ANYTHING he's saying on. Seems to me he read the cartoon and tried to think up ways to argue with it just through pure conjecture.

But to break it down, here's a number of stupid, stupid things he says:

> As mentioned, Google banks on the idea that browser tabs are leaking memory. Thankfully there is an easy fix for this. Either use IE8, or when loading a demanding web page, do so in a new browser window, which operates on a separate process.

Ah yes, GREAT idea. Pick IE, the most insecure browser in existence, or open other browser windows? What year does he think we live in? 2002? Who the hell is going to be opening extra instances just for "demanding web pages"?

> Next up, a figure wonders if it would be great to have a new browser. Apparently, it could be great. On the other hand designers, that will have yet another browser to test their pages against, wouldn't be delighted. Neither would plugin developers such as Adobe, Sun and Microsoft that would have yet another environment to support for their Flash, Java and Media Player + Silverlight plugins.

So apparently designing a new browser is BAD because it means web designers have another browser to test on. We just have to suck it up and deal with imperfect browsers because the poor devs shouldn't have to deal with a new browser. This guy must also hate Linux because it's to much work for software designers to have to deal with another OS.

By the way, did anyone else find it weird that he mentions using Internet Explorer a few times and brings up Silverlight, a technology that frankly no one is actually using? I notice Firefox gets no mention at all, either.

Being bored, I googled him. [http://www.linkedin.com/pub/3/2A5/78B](http://www.linkedin.com/pub/3/2A5/78B)

> Georgios Kasselakis&#8217;s Education

    *
      Aristoteleion Panepistimion Thessalonikis

      Computer Science 2003 &#8212; 2007

      Activities and Societies:
          Co founded the Hellenic Student Computer Science Company, **Microsoft Student Partner**

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-09-18
> **sharifi14 said:**

By native, it is meant it is binary compatible or targets the OS. It is a bit blurry now, but since Wine provides a compatibility layer for an entirely incompatible platform, anything that runs on Wine is not native.

The look and feel is not a factor at all.

> **sharifi14 said:**
> 
My question is, do people think Chrome will be treated the same as Google Earth and Picasa, or will it flow more with the whole GUI of the operating system, much like Chrome does on XP and Vista.

Chrome doesn't flow with the whole GUI in Windows...

### Post by FuturePilot on 2008-09-18
Let's step back for a second and forget about toolkits, GUIs, Interfaces, DE Integration, etc.. I'm speaking strictly of the binaries.

```
/opt/picasa/wine/drive_c/Program Files/Picasa2$ ls
buttons    Picasa2.exe     PicasaMediaDetector.exe  qtsupport.dll  update
cdautorun  Picasa2.scr     PicasaUpdate.exe         runtime        web
i18n       picasai18n.dll  plugins                  Uninstall.exe

```
There's Picasa. Now I know those are not native Linux binaries. That's the same thing as the Windows version. Yes it runs on Wine which is native to Linux. That's where the native-ness stops. Only Wine is native to Linux. The actual program Picasa is not.

---

### Post by sharifi14 on 2008-09-18
> **LaRoza said:**
> By native, it is meant it is binary compatible or targets the OS. It is a bit blurry now, but since Wine provides a compatibility layer for an entirely incompatible platform, anything that runs on Wine is not native.

The look and feel is not a factor at all.
Thanks for the to-the-point definition! That seems to be the paragraph I've been looking for!

> **LaRoza said:**
> Chrome doesn't flow with the whole GUI in Windows...

It looks unique enough to differentiate itself from the rest of the programs on the OS, but consistent enough to make you feel like you're still using Windows.

### Post by gnomeuser on 2008-09-19
> **simtaalo said:**
> a minimalist gtk theme is what id like from linux chrome

And that is what you will get:

[http://dev.chromium.org/developers/faq](http://dev.chromium.org/developers/faq)

> 
Q. Does Chromium on Linux use GTK or Qt?
A. We're too early in the port to have incorporated a toolkit.  Most of the custom drawing goes through a library called Skia, which is comparable to Cairo in that it draws lines and rectangles but not buttons and checkboxes.

Many people have strong opinions about the toolkit, and part of the reason it's so divisive is because both libraries are quite capable of meeting Chromium's needs. In fact, because most of Chromium is just custom rendering for showing a web page -- for example, even the popup of an HTML <select> control is custom-drawn by WebKit -- we anticipate the only real places the toolkit will be visible are in the way some form controls look and in various dialogs like the preferences and "save as" dialogs.

With all of that said, the plan is to use GTK. It's not due to any dislike of Qt, but just because there's more experience on the team with GTK and it matches the existing Firefox dependency on Linux. Please keep calm. :)


---

### Post by Closed_Port on 2008-09-19
> **LaRoza said:**
> By native, it is meant it is binary compatible or targets the OS.

Binary compatible with what?
And how is it meant? Reference needed.

> **LaRoza said:**
> 
It is a bit blurry now, but since Wine provides a compatibility layer for an entirely incompatible platform, anything that runs on Wine is not native.

How is it entirely incompatible?
What about programs written with cross-platform toolkits?
What about programs especially written for wine?

---

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-09-19
> **Closed_Port said:**
> Binary compatible with what?
And how is it meant? Reference needed.

What do you mean? Windows use the PE format. Linux uses the ELF format. Two different binary formats. Code compiled for Windows makes different system calls.

> 
How is it entirely incompatible?

Its binary code makes system calls specific to DOS or Windows. It is an entirely different format.

> 
What about programs written with cross-platform toolkits?

The Windows version of GTK isn't the same as the Linux version. They have the same API, that is it.

> 
What about programs especially written for wine?

Wine provides a compatibility layer between Linux and Windows. Anything written "for wine" is written for Windows.

---

### Post by Closed_Port on 2008-09-19
> **LaRoza said:**
> 
The Windows version of GTK isn't the same as the Linux version. They have the same API, that is it.

Wine is an Open Source implementation of the Windows API on top of X, OpenGL, and Unix.
[http://www.winehq.org/](http://www.winehq.org/)

---

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-09-19
> **Closed_Port said:**
> Wine is an Open Source implementation of the Windows API on top of X, OpenGL, and Unix.
[http://www.winehq.org/](http://www.winehq.org/)

Yes? So? What does that have to do with anything I said besides a rewording of my definiation of wine?

Oh, and while we are at it, you should really look up a definition of binary compatible. It's not what you seem to think.

Anyway, this conversation seems increasingly pointless, as people seem hellbend on even defining software that is written for a library that is native on linux as being non-native.

And just to be clear: I can certainly understand people having issues with software being ported by using wine, but having issues because of some perceived and vaguely defined non-nativeness always strikes me as uninformed and, to put it bluntly, rather silly.

### Post by enlightenment now on 2008-09-22
> **StOoZ said:**
> I've just seen this :
[http://www.ubuntugeek.com/how-to-install-chromium-google-chrome-in-ubuntu-using-deb-package.html](http://www.ubuntugeek.com/how-to-install-chromium-google-chrome-in-ubuntu-using-deb-package.html)

now , so there is a linux version or not?

There is also this: [Google Chrome Build Instructions for Linux]("http://cafelinux.org/forum/index.php/topic,1931.0.html")

The answer is yes & no, you have to look at it from Google's perspective they are asking Linux users to jump in and help develop it for Linux.

They have given you all the tools you need. Now it is up to you to help develop it.

If it hasn't occurred to anyone yet the answer to why is obvious. Google is using this as a recruitment tool for Linux Developers. The ones that sit back and complain "why haven't they released a Linux version first" fall on deaf ears. The Linux User/Developers that say hey this is cool, they have given me all the tools I need to build a Linux version of Google Chrome. These are the ones that Google is hoping for. This does not mean the Linux Developer would accept the job from Google if offered but it would open a door for Google to take notice and consideration, and perhaps contact the developer(s).

Also, if you interested I have started a Google Chrome Social Group here at Ubuntu Forums, if your interested please join, 
[http://ubuntuforums.org/group.php?groupid=286](http://ubuntuforums.org/group.php?groupid=286)
